B006TQUNX8|5.0|Dorian Gray is absurdly good-looking. Utterly infatuated, Basil Hallward captures Dorian's youthful beauty in a portrait.<br /><br />While posing for this portrait, Dorian meets Basil's friend, Lord Henry Wotton, who charmingly preaches hedonism. Lord Henry's efforts to corrupt Dorian work quickly. Convinced that youth and beauty are the only things worth having in life, Dorian looks upon his finished portrait with cruel jealousy. His image will never grow old, but he will.<br /><br />One wish sends the plot of the novel into action: Dorian wishes the portrait would age, while he stays youthful.<br /><br />Enter a lover whose heart Dorian breaks. Her despair coerces her to suicide. With her death on his conscience, he notices an evil glint in the eye of his portrait. Over the years, Basil's painting begins to age. Dorian sabotages reputations and even commits murder, all the while looking as young and beautiful as he did on the day his portrait was painted. However, the portrait-a grotesque reminder of his double life-documents every immoral act.<br /><br />Focusing on duplicity and aestheticism, The Picture of Dorian Gray is a captivating and cinematic tale. Oscar Wilde's knack for dialogue is what made him a famous playwright and parts of Dorian Gray are rich with clever play-like exchanges.<br /><br />Lord Henry's wit is exceptionally tantalizing. But he doesn't seem to take himself too seriously, whereas Dorian adopts everything Lord Henry preaches with the conviction of a Southern Baptist. He's basically just a dumb pretty person that everyone coddles despite the fact that he's a terrible human being.<br /><br />**Side note**<br />The Picture of Dorian Gray is Oscar Wilde's only novel. The book was scandalous when it was released in 1891 because of clear homoerotic undertones. Dorian's lovers are both men and women. Though not overtly sexual, Basil's infatuation with Dorian is questionable. Wilde himself had an affair with Robert Ross.

B0061JPYX2|4.0|Listening to this recording is such a bitter-sweet experience. Amy Winehouse had the voice and the musical talent to build a career for the ages. Unlike so many pop divas, her genius was built to last. She could have become an Ella Fitzgerald-like figure -- a towering icon. If only. If only the self-destructive part of her psyche could have been brought under control. You read the liner notes of this CD and you see the photos of the intrinsically lovely but deeply damaged person -- you see the signs of personal destructiveness on her body. And you sigh for what might have been.<br /><br />I don't think this was Amy's best work. I didn't love all the arrangements. But it shouldn't have mattered. She should have had many years to do better and better, to nurture and grow that talent, to fulfill that huge potential.<br /><br />What I do like about the choice of music is the way she was consciously placing herself as a link in the chain of a grand musical tradition. 0307593312|3.0|This review recently appeared on nipponinitiative.org.    I first heard of 1Q84 by Haruki Murakami while listening one day to NPR. The person mentioning the book said something like, \\"If you want to know more about how Japanese people think, 1Q84 is a must-read.\\" So I ordered the book and began to read it, all 925 pages of it. That's right--925 pages. Other than the Bible, it is the longest book I've ever read. Even the New York Times lamented the length of 1Q84, noting \\"Mr. Murakami's determination to describe, inventory and echo just about everything that he chooses to mention.\\"    Fair enough, but the question remains: Does 1Q84 deliver with insights into the mind of Japanese people? If so, what exactly does it say about their worldview?    1Q84 is the story of two people, Aomame and Tengo, separated during childhood but reunited as young adults drawn into a world heavily under the influence of powerful \\"Little People.\\" In the world of 1Q84, Little People, who magically enter the world through the mouth of a dead goat, control weather and other natural phenomena. They manipulate both circumstances and people, expressing anger, issuing warnings, turning some into their agents--all this without the benefit of omnipotence or moral anchors. \\"We do not know if the so-called Little People are good or evil, \\" someone remarks. \\"But the important thing is that, whether they are good or evil, light or shadow, whenever they begin to exert their power, a compensatory force comes into being.\\"    If 1Q84 reveals the worldview of modern Japanese, this exchange shows how central of a role Shinto continues to play in hearts and minds throughout the land of the Rising Sun, in spite of so many now claiming to have turned away from religion toward more urban ways of thinking. Shinto, also known as \\"kami no michi\\" (the way of kami), embraces the existence of divine forces that, like \\"Little People,\\" are neither good nor evil, moral nor immoral: \\"kami are manifold; some kami are noble, while others are lowly, some are strong, whereas others are weak; again, some kami are good, while others are evil.\\"1 Once again, kami are like \\"Little People,\\" everywhere and potentially in everything, including humans.    As in the world of 1Q84, which drew in Aomame and Tengo, the world wherein kami exist is unpredictable and dangerous. It is a world wherein the rules of ethics and morality may not apply. Instead, right thinking and right action are determined by \\"somatically enacted feelings\\"2; in other words, by conforming body and attitudes to the way things are and then acting accordingly. Such a world provides no absolute moral or physical safe harbor.    In 1Q84, the lives of Aomame and Tengo illustrate fear, hopelessness, despair, and a desire to escape from reality. If Murakami's portrayal accurately illustrates the worldview of modern Japanese with all of its vestiges of Shinto, then his work also stands as a call for answers that will truly serve the deepest needs of the hearts of his dear countrymen. At the Nippon Initiative, of course, we believe the worldview of biblical Christianity offers real and meaningful solutions to the world of Shinto and 1Q84. Strangely enough, Murakami himself opens that door as the story of 1Q84 unfolds. More about this in Part 2 of this review.  PART 2: The world of 1Q84 proves to be dangerous and unpredictable, a world that lends itself to fear, hopelessness, and despair. It is no wonder that author Haruki Murakami portrays the main characters of 1Q84, Aomame and Tengo, as driven to escape the uncertainty and threat it poses to them. Early in the 925-page tome, Tengo explains how he often retreated to mathematics, a realm of \\"infinite consistency,\\" in order to \\"escape from the troublesome cage of reality.\\"  To Tengo:    The world governed by numerical expression was, for him, a legitimate and always safe hiding place. As long as he stayed in that world, he could forget or ignore the rules and burdens forced upon him bythe real world.   However, over time, Tengo discovered just as much dissatisfaction with the world of mathematics as he had experienced with the \\"burdens\\" of the real world: \\"In elementary and middle school, Tengo was utterly absorbed by the world of mathematics. Its clarity and absolute freedom enthralled him, and he also needed them to survive. Once he entered adolescence, however, he began to feel increasingly that this might not be enough. There was no problem as long as he was visiting the world of math, but when he returned to the real world (as return he must), he found himself in the same miserable cage. Nothing had improved. Rather, his shackles felt even heavier. So then, what good was mathematics? Wasn't it just a temporary means of escape that made his real-life situation even worse?\\"    In these lines, Murakami puts his finger on a dilemma faced by so many Japanese people. On one hand, there appears to be such an attraction in the hearts and minds of Japanese people to order and predictability. These are the things that preserve harmony in a world that otherwise has so much potential for chaos. On the other hand, the chaos inherent in the Shinto and Buddhist worldviews stubbornly persists without any real solution. It is like a pair of shackles on the soul that, as Tengo indicated, \\"feel even heavier\\" when he returned to the real world.   So how does Tengo deal with this dilemma? He escapes to the world of story:\\"The role of a story was, in the broadest terms, to transpose a single problem into another form. Depending on the nature and direction of the problem, a solution could be suggested in the narrative. Tengo would return to the real world with that suggestion in hand. It was like a piece of paper bearing the indecipherable text of a magic spell. At times it lacked coherence and served no immediate practical purpose. But it would contain a possibility. Somehow he might be able to decipher the spell. That possibility would gently warm his heart from within.\\"   In other words, Tengo decided that if there were going to be any solution to the shackles on his soul, it would have to be found in some alternative way of understanding how and why the world works the way that it does, along with what place he personally has in it.    In Part 3 of this review, we will look at one of the alternatives--the worldview of biblical Christianity in contrast with the worldview of 1Q84. We will see once again that Murakami's novel opens the door to the possibility of a biblical worldview, coming closer to laying a foundation for its narrative than the author may have ever imagined.  PART 3: The storyline of 1Q84 centers on the main characters being drawn into another realm of existence. It is a realm dominated by \\"Little People\\", kami-like beings full of mystery and mischief, and therefore unpredictable and dangerous. As it turns out, life in 1Q84, for Aomame and Tengo (and several other characters besides) proves to be utterly unattractive, offering no relief for the shackles Tengo felt on his soul, as described in Part 2 of this review.   Author Haruki Murakami's storyline taps into a view of reality similar to one found in biblical Christianity. Like 1Q84, the Christian worldview also asserts the existence of a realm that intersects and interacts with the ordinary realm in which we live. Only instead of being ruled by \\"Little People\\", the parallel realm of the Christian worldview is under the rule of the living, Creator God, an eternal King over all. In the New Testament book of Matthew, Jesus refers to this realm as \\"the kingdom of the heavens.\\"    One of the many differences between the operation of the realm of 1Q84 and the kingdom of the heavens is this: in 1Q84, Aomame and Tengo leave this realm to enter into the realm of the Little People; in the biblical account, the kingdom of the heavens penetrates into this realm. In other words, no one leaves this world to encounter the kingdom of the heavens; the kingdom of the heavens comes to us.    Another difference involves the matters of danger and despair. Unlike the world of 1Q84, encounters with the kingdom of the heavens are encounters with blessing, divine reversals of misfortune, and life to the full. All of these, by the way, are promised by Jesus Christ to those who interact with the kingdom of the heavens through him by faith in hm. This is not to say that the biblical worldview offers \\"escape\\" from the dangers and daily grind of the physical world. Instead it offers the other-world power and resources of the kingdom of the heavens to cope with life in this realm. It also offers meaning to explain the existence of evil and suffering and make them more bearable. You might say, in its penetration into this realm, the kingdom of the heavens offers sustaining hope and help for people whose lives are racked with weakness, pain, and loss.   One summary of how this works can be found in Matthew 5:1-12, Bible verses otherwise known as The Beatitudes. For example, Matthew 5:5 says: \\"Blessed are the meek, for they shall inherit the earth.\\" Meekness would not ordinarily be valued in a world that is as competitive and power-hungry as ours. However, the words of Matthew 5:5 indicate that, in the operation of the kingdom of the heavens here on earth, meekness, not power-mongering, is the path to blessing.    In 1Q84, Tengo and Aomame become obsessed with escaping the realm in which they find themselves and each other. They return in the end to the ordinary realm from which they came, where the deep longings of their hearts will continue to go unmet. By contrast, in the biblical Christian worldview, whenever people live in proper relation to the kingdom of the heavens through faith in Jesus Christ, life in this world not only becomes bearable; it becomes abundantly meaningful and satisfying.    For more on what it means to live in proper relation to the kingdom of the heavens through faith in Jesus Christ, go to nipponinitiative.org. You will find a link to email us at the end of part 3 of the review.  1 Robert S. Gall in \\"Kami and Daimn: A Cross-cultural Reflection of what is divine,\\" p. 64.  2 Ibid, p. 67.

B00005B36H|5.0|Lateralus was the first song I heard from this album and the first TOOL song I had ever listened to. As soon as Lateralus's opening was finished and the song burst forth into its beautiful and intricate mathematics based infrastructure, a slow smile creeped upon my face. The first thing I noticed was that TOOL was unlike any band I had ever heard (and I listen to a wide variety of music from alternative and progressive to metal, posthardcore, and classical/orchestra). I couldn't and still cannot pinpoint exactly what it is that makes TOOL any different than the innumerable amount of bands that exist today. It isn't specifically the genius and raw emotional talent of Maynard James Keenan or his chilling and mind opening lyrics. No, he isn't the sole reason the band has climbed to the pinnacle of the rock throne in many listener's minds. Nor is it Danny Carey's uncanny ability to flawlessly switch time signatures with minimal effort while decisively directing the flow of the song without you even realizing what happened. Adam Jones' guitar playing ebbs and flows and nestles itself directly into the listener's soul. The way the guitar is utilized by Jones speaks in and of itself. The guitar gently guides the listener to the inner most parts of his mind as he tries to answer questions about himself and the universe that he previously had no knowledge of, and behind all the layers the band meticulously works through is Justin Chancellor'a skillful bass playing that shows he knows how to interest the listener through deep and powerful bass notes that work with the guitar to move the mind to wherever it wants to wander yet are independent enough to stand on their own.<br /><br />None of these things are particularly unique. Musicianship and talent shine in several bands, however, when these four guys get together and work on music, a beautiful world is created that few others, if any, can even compare to. Indeed, TOOL is greater than the sum of its parts, TOOL is more thanJUST Keenan, Jones, Carey, or Chancellor individually and it shows so well with the experience known as Lateralus. The beauty of TOOL is that the band members never release anything until they are comfortable that they've created a masterful and layered, complex cohesive piece of art, for that is the only way to describe it. They never cease to draw me in and keep me mesmerized while I whittle away the hours thinking and exploring the reality around me.<br /><br />Now, on to the cd itself. I am heavily biased about this cd because I bought Lateralus while on a college visit and I had no means to listen to the cd until I got home. Little did I know that a blizzard would hit and cause the van I was in to roll upside down into the median. I was sitting down calmly sifting through the unique artwork that TOOL includes with their album releases when next thing I knew we were upside down in the snow. After opening the door and climbing out, I was greeted by an EMT who had asked me if everyone was ok. Everyone climbed out and we proceeded to scour the wreckage for our belongings. My friend shouted \\"Hey, your cd's okay!\\" to which I went over to him and inspected Lateralus. It had one scratch and some dirt on it, but otherwise it was flawless. We all gathered our luggage and went to a Department of Highways station where we waited for another ride home. The mud is still on my cd case. I refuse to remove it as it is a constant reminder of the experience I went through. After arriving home the first thing I did was make a cup of coffee, relax in a hot bath, and put my cd in. I was absolutely shocked.<br /><br />The first song \\"The Grudge\\" exploded into an energetic scale of peaceful aggressiveness. TOOL couldn't have chosen a better way to open a cd. TOOL does what they do best on this song, they build. The song constantly builds off of itself, repeating and changing crucial sections till they sound vaguely familiar but noticeably unique.<br /><br />Then I was treated to \\"EonBlue Apocalypse\\". A one minute song that has deep emotion in its few notes. The perfect way to settle the mood for the next song while still keeping the idea of the previous aggression from \\"The Grudge\\".<br /><br />\\"The Patient\\" is the next journey I get to travel. The slow melodic pace of the song trots along as it slowly builds up and peaks, then treks back down to a quiet valley of personal experience and pain. This is one of my favorite songs on Lateralus as its peaceful at parts with little more than the guitar and a few light taps from Danny. The Patient is very personal to Maynard as the lyrics are based on the experience his mother had to endure for several years.<br /><br />Next comes \\"Mantra\\" an odd track but one that serves its purpose well. TOOL puts a lot of songs in their releases that may seem like filler material. Whether or not it is is up to the listener. I personally believe that it's one that can be skipped without missing anything from Lateralus but others may not feel the same. Supposedly \\"Mantra\\" is Maynard lightly squeezing his cat and slowing down the recording, however that's just what I've heard.<br /><br />Moving along I get to one of the best riffs in any song I've ever heard. The bass lines for \\"Schism\\" are rough and powerful while the guitar oddly skips along to lead the procession. A beautifully unique and complex song that draws in with the opening riff and slowly allows the listener to experience what he/she wants from the song. I've listened to \\"Schism\\" so many times and it truly is different each time as I notice subtle differences in parts that I previously thought to have had completely memorized. Schism's lyrics are very poetic and warn of tragedy by man against man.<br /><br />\\"Parabol\\" is where I truly became invested into Lateralus. Sitting there in the relaxing warmth of the water, with eyes closed and mind opened, I heard a pleading prelude into the definition of humanity. \\"Parabol\\" is the perfect song that sets the moodwonderfully for the next track \\"Parabola\\". It's little more than Maynard singing as if he's a man distantly preparing himself for a message by slowly holding out notes almost as if he's humming, and Adam playing the music that this man would hear in his mind as he mentally contrives his opus.<br /><br />This song has personal history with me now after what I went through before listening. \\"Parabola\\" is a man's tale about who he is as a human and how he sees his life. The lyrics that have stuck with me the moment I heard them are \\"This body holding me, reminds me of my own mortality/ embrace this moment remember we are eternal all this pain is an illusion\\" and \\"Recognize this as a holy gift and celebrate this chance to be alive and breathing\\". I had come so close to death in that very day that I stopped and thought about this message, this song provided me with a cathartic experience. It is so easy to forget what truly matters in life that constant reminders are needed to break us out of our personal worlds in order to experience reality as it truly is.<br /><br />\\"Ticks and Leeches\\" is the exact opposite of \\"Parabola\\" and that contrast is what makes the placement of this track such a great decision. Its harsh and raw with Maynard belting out rough vocals mostly the whole song and Danny progressively getting more and more intense as the song goes on.<br /><br />\\"Lateralus\\" is a truly unique piece of art. The Fibonacci sequence is a prominent theme throughout this song. The rhythm follows the first few numbers of the Fibonacci sequence in ascending then descending order. The Fibonacci sequence is a naturally occurring sequence of numbers where the next number in the sequence is the addition of the previous two numbers. For example, the first numbers (and the one that the chorus's syllables are based on) are 0, 1, 1, 2, 3, 5, 8. 0 and 1 are 1, 1 and 1 are 2, 1 and 2 are 3 so on and so forth. So, in relation to the song, the chorus with the number of syllables in parentheses is, \\"Black (1)/ then (1)/ white are (2)/ all I see (3)/ in my infancy (5)/ red and yellow then came to be (8)/ reaching out to me (5)/ lets me see (3)\\". Later in the song Maynard sings about how we should \\"ride the spiral to the end/ and may just go where no one's been\\". When the Fibonacci sequence is graphed it forms a spiral that gets progressively larger. It has no end. This is what Maynard is talking about, human beings exploring and discovering new ideas and inventions, going ever further mentally and physically for the sake of intelligence and design.<br /><br />The next three songs, \\"Disposition\\", \\"Reflection\\", and \\"Triad\\", are considered a trio and often played back-to-back during live shows. Often I will find myself confused as to when the song changes to the next when I listen to them. All three are beautiful in their own way and often mellow out my mood, the perfect songs to somberly ponder upon perplexities as precipitation pours down. \\"Disposition\\" and \\"Reflection\\" are the slow somber songs, whereas \\"Triad\\" picks up the intensity. \\"Triad\\" is an AMAZING guitar song and Adam Jones shows here that he knows exactly what he's doing to his equipment whenever he touches it.<br /><br />\\"Faiip de Oiad\\" means \\"Voice of God\\". It is a recording of a man ranting about aliens while TOOL accompanies the frantic feeling of the song.<br /><br />That was my experience with Lateralus and it is why I consider it one of the best written albums of this generation. Hands down, TOOL is at the top of their artistry career and I look forward to their next exploration. (All lyrics were taken from TOOL's website as TOOL does not add lyrics with their albums for artistic reasons)

The argument is, your arms will fatigue over time.  True... IF that is the way you hold it the entire time driving.  But you will quickly find you can rest it on your lap, and even better yet, lay back in a recliner, get comfortable, rest it on your lap and race for literally hours. So to me, being unmounted and being very light weight, is now the way to go.<br /><br />There is another considerable advantage the wheel has over mounted wheels, and that is its ability to control the car by moving the wheel laterally WITHOUT rotating the device.  This adds a subtle layer of control.  It may hamper someone new to the wheel, but once you become familiar with the nuance, you will have a slight advantage over a mounted wheel, at least in respect to control.<br /><br />Now. . .there are a couple of legitimate drawbacks.Since it IS unmounted, it is difficult in the middle of a race to have both hands free, even for a second. As I said, you can perch it on your lap for a second or two, but it's difficult since the slightest non-horizontal angle will make the car drift off course. The good news is, the brake and acceleration paddles allow it to rest on a flat surface upright.  To save weight, it is corded (not wireless, no batteries) and does not have force feedback. And since you, more or less, turn it in the air, there is also absolutely no turning resistance.<br /><br />Yes there are drawbacks, no force feedback, must have control if it at all times during a race, but if you can live with these small quibbles, I highly recommend it.

B005LAIGN2|1.0|Over twenty five years after the fact and Maverick has become a drunk who makes hasty decisions under the influence, Goose has become a bit more reckless and still puts women before anything else, and Iceman's role has been reduced significantly as his smug arrogance is only felt in a handful of lines. What's that you say? Red Tails isn't the urban retelling of Top Gun? Well, you could have fooled me. It's not that it makes much difference though. No matter how you look at it, Red Tails doesn't really have much of anything to offer.<br /><br />At the peak of World War II, African American pilots are considered the lowest of the low. They're considered to be incapable of performing their tasks to their country to the fullest and are given leftover missions that don't even qualify as scraping the bottom of the barrel. The Tuskegee training program is no different as the entire squadron is mostly reduced to shooting down trucks, trains, and perhaps a cow every now and then. That is until Colonel A.J. Bullard (Terrence Howard) succeeds in letting the higher ups give his men a chance and they take full advantage of the opportunity.<br /><br />Red Tails feels really cheesy as soon as that quote is shown in the opening scene. The movie has a $58 million budget, but it certainly doesn't feel that way considering the opening credits. The amalgamation of planes ripping through the sky and tearing each other to pieces feels like a distraction or a cover up for the rest of your senses. Try to pay attention to the credits, how plain they are, and how cheap they look. That along with the score that feels like it was ripped straight out of a stereotypical action film from the 90s doesn't really seem like the type of tone they were going for here. The acting isn't much better as stiffness and monotony seem to be what they were aiming for. The Tuskegee airmen do begin to get a bit more comfortable in their roles as the movie progresses. David Oyelowo takes Lightning the furthest as far as Easy's (Nate Parker) squad goes, but they certainly seem their best in the face of tragedy. Terrence Howard has a few great moments, as well. Specifically his \\"highest expectations\\" scene he shares with Joe \\"Lightning\\" Little. But it isn't enough to save a second-rate film.<br /><br />The script is very dry. I don't mean dry humor I mean about as pleasant as trying to listen to somebody with a mouthful of saltine crackers. It feels so stale and again contributes to that 90s atmosphere I mentioned earlier. Keep an ear out for the Americans and how third grade they sound. The dialogue along with the monotonous tone spread out amongst every actor in the film makes everyone come off as a robot. \\"These cows are armed,\\" is a line that's actually used in the movie. There are a few lines that are almost decent. Winky's (Leslie Odom Jr) line where he says, \\"Every time I close that canopy I feel like I'm closing the lid to my own coffin,\\" is surprisingly good. It gives the momentary belief that things may turn around, but they never do. Smokey's (Ne-Yo) \\"colored\\" conversation at the bar is the most amusing thing Red Tails has to offer, as well. There are many lame attempts at humor that just make you groan. Black Jesus is perhaps the worst joke of all, considering how things turn out for Deke (Marcus T. Paulk).<br /><br />I was expecting Red Tails to at least offer a fair amount of eye candy, but the special effects weren't really all that impressive at all. There's a train explosion early on in the film that's been shown in nearly every trailer and TV spot. It's pretty much on the same scale as the train crash in Super 8, but it isn't nearly as spectacular. That's kind of weird considering Super 8 was $8 million dollars cheaper than Red Tails when it comes to its budget. What ruined it for me was that sloppy zoom-in effect that's relied on as a crutch the entire movie. The Tuskegee airmen will be in the midst of a heated air battle with the Germans. The camera would pan up to try and capture the action, but it would zoom-in (or out on a few occasions) nearly every time to try and show you who should be keeping track of. It just came off as sloppy camera work to me, as if they didn't have the proper camera placement for the scene and tried to adjust it accordingly. There are several things that don't really add up in the movie either. One of the main ones for me was that at one point, Easy tells everyone that there's no time to celebrate a completed mission and to return to base because they're low on fuel. But can you guess what they do in the very next scene? They showboat and celebrate. Not listening to your superiors and still getting praised for it is an overbearing theme in the movie, as well.<br /><br />George Lucas fought for 23 years to get this made, but after viewing it you'll more than likely be convinced that the man has grown senile over the years and that retirement from Hollywood is the best thing for him. The turnout for the screening was insane and you can bet that Red Tails will more than likely make a killing at the box office, but there's no way in hell it passes as a good or even decent piece of cinema. Amateur cinematography, a boring script, terrible acting, and the fate of certain characters being extremely predictable (you can guess Lightning's fate around the time things start getting serious with Sophia), Red Tails is the equivalent of a stand-up act that gets booed off the stage. It is just awful. I was left wishing the entire movie was just Cuba Gooding Jr lifting a pipe to his lips, pulling it away, and pretending to smoke the entire film. If it was just those clips strung together with no dialogue and that dubstep track from the TV spot in the background, I could have at least gotten a good laugh out of it.it would zoom-in (or out on a few occasions) nearly every time to try and show you who should be keeping track of. 0062082353|4.0|\\"American Sniper\\" is really two stories in one: Chris Kyle's journey to becoming the United State's most prolific military sniper ever; and a surprisingly frank account of a couple's marriage strained by the separation caused by the war and Kyle's commitment to the Teams.  Interwoven into the story of Kyle's deployments overseas and his brief times back at home are short sections supplied by Taya Kyle documenting her feelings and her side of the story.  The combination serves to provide a poignant contrast between the brutalities faced by warriors in battle and the worry and emotions ripping through their families at home.  I get the feeling that Chris Kyle is a man of few words because his descriptions of events and memories tend to be on the spare side. But the author's (Jim DeFelice) ability to capture the tone and meaning of Kyle's stories is excellent. You can pick up the cadence and rhythm of Kyle's speech with the one-liners leaving you chuckling. With a few haunting exceptions, there is almost always a comical or humorous side aspect to Kyle's stories.  For those who want to pick up a lot of detail about being a SEAL or a sniper, you're not going to get much. There are some nuggets in the stories that you can probably only mine if you have some knowledge of combat and shooting. For example, you won't find a reference to a Mil-Dot anywhere. But, Kyle talks about using a tree behind some insurgents to help him on an extremely long-distance shot. Shooters will know what he is talking about. Others probably won't get the significance of the tree. (His longest shot, by the way, was 2,100 yards taken to kill a bad guy getting ready to launch a rocket at an Army convoy).  He fairly brushes over his experiences during BUD/S and Hell Week, telling the reader that the process is well documented in other sources.  There is a bit of treasure on p. 126 (Geared Up) where he does give some good descriptions of his gear and the reasoning behind his likes and dislikes on each piece.  Likes? .45 over the 9mm; belt holster over the drop-leg holster. Depending on the mission he would carry an M-4, a Mk-11, a .300 Win Mag and, later, a .338 Lapua (which he used to the make 2,100 yard shot).  I found the book extremely fast-paced and I found the addition of Taya's sections valuable in making  this a very memorable account of their lives. I easily read it in a few sessions.  God bless Texas if the Lone Star State keeps producing heroes like Chris Kyle (and his buddy Marcus Luttrell). He doesn't mince words and calls the situation like he sees it. His experience gives him some very simple, yet insightful, answers to situations that seem to perplex politicians. He is a bad-ass and he hands out copious amounts of kudos for others who are bad-asses. Not surprisingly, he has few nice things to say about cowards and those unwilling to commit.  Great book. Entertaining and worth the read.

0446563048|4.0|As a disclosure, I am not a Zappos customer.  Going into this, I had read about Zappos culture from business magazines and knew it was something different from conventional business culture.  I tend to favor books written by their founder.  They offer terrific insight into what drives them to create successful businesses that hopefully the reader can take away.  On that end, this book does not disappoint and really does inspire you to take a different look at running a company.  The title of this book also suggests that the culture at Zappos is driven by the theme of delivering happiness.  I would have liked to have it emphasize the warehouse logistics and technology of delivering shoes and that would have been more of an interesting read for me personally.  But in the end, it drives the point home that this is a company built on keeping customers happy and keeping employees happy.  The book chronicles the author's journey from school to founding his first company, Link Exchange, to a brief stint as a venture capitalist which led to discovering, investing, saving and ultimately selling Zappos to Amazon.  In that process, the book also goes through the author's ethos of profit, passion and purpose at Zappos.  Zappos was initially started by Nick who called his site ShoeSite before renaming it Zappos and brought in Fred as a Co-Founder.  The author's role at Zappos transformed from a stay out of the way angel investor to a hands-on, risk the house in order to save the company journey that at times was a month or two from running out of money and closing its doors.  One thing that should not escape the reader is that Zappos was started at the height of the dot come boom, survived the dot come crash, and come to survive the 2008 financial crisis, which says a lot about what good customer service can accomplish and the author's leadership.  Some minor parting points I would have liked to have read about, not to take away from an otherwise well written book  1.  I would have liked to read more on Nick's role (eventually he parted ways)  2.  Liked to have learn more about WHSKY, the system that ran the Kentucky warehouse  3.  I think if Zappos had gotten into books, they would have been a formidable competitor to Amazon.
1451627289|5.0|Stephen King may be the greatest living pure story teller in existence today.  I've read almost all of his work, and while there are instances that I didn't particularly enjoy, the writing was still engaging and the underlying story still masterfully presented.  And talk about prolific!  When King decides to write a book (which he frequently does), he doesn't automatically crank out 250-300 page money printers.  Generally, you get your money's worth with King, as in this 900 page door stop (though, admittedly, the spacing and type actually justify about 700 pages; still a healthy tome).<br /><br />Most commonly known for his horror stories, this effort demonstrates King's range; because, at his heart, he is a pure storyteller, regardless of the genre.  King could write science fiction or romance and be equally adept at captivating his audience with his ability to spin a yarn.  I've opined in the past on my preference for his efforts that seek to enter the human mind and demonstrate the horror in each of us.  He does this by creating realistic circumstances that we can all relate to, and demonstrating how terrifying events can ensue.  He does this without resort to corporeal beings that have become increasingly silly and absurd over time.  After all, isn't reality so much more potentially horrifying than the \\"over the top\\", stupid happenings in so many of King's works?<br /><br />Duma Key is a perfect example.  The first two-thirds of the book are a fine example of King's ability to get inside the head of a damaged human being and write masterfully.  The final third of the book is simply too bizarre for words and, in my opinion, ruined what could have been an excellent book.  The Gunslinger books are just unreadable (at least to me).  Even in books such as The Dome, with unbelievable and impossible sets of events, King's ability to examine the working of the human mind (especially in those that happen to be defective) is what sets him apart from his contemporaries.<br /><br />While this novel certainly has aspects of horror (the type I prefer), it is essentially a time travel story, with all of the questions and complexities inherent in such an effort.  Our protagonist is introduced to a time portal and urged to make use of it for the purpose of preventing the Kennedy assassination.  What follows is pure entertainment.  This is one of the best King novels I've read in quite some time.tainly has aspects of horror (the type I prefer), it is essentially a time travel story, with all of the questions and complexities inherent in such an effort.  Our protagonist is introduced to a time portal and urged to make use of it for the purpose of preventing the Kennedy assassination.  What follows is pure entertainment.  This is one of the best King novels I've read in quite some time.

1451648537|4.0|The constant refrain through the book is the exceptional insight and force of Steve Jobs to make things happen according to his direction and boundaries, along with the obnoxious behavior that accompanied that life. On both fronts, he had few peers. The incidents of horrible behavior sure piled up. If the author had taken it easy on the latter, the book would not have worked, and this reader is grateful for the cooperation of Jobs himself. For a man whose work output was so public, his private life was indeed highly private and Isaacson was able to get behind the curtain enough to make the book work effectively.  Was the author too favorable on the vision, uniqueness and quality of Apple's products and Jobs' own role? That has already been debated extensively and will continue to be. To me, it doesn't matter that much, and opinion can be filtered. The better part is the story behind some of the decision making, and I would willingly have accepted more.  As a businessman, instead of a product visionary, the Pixar story may actually have been more impressive than that of Jobs the Apple CEO. Much of Apple's success as a company, such as the manufacturing engine in China and all the back-office stuff, followed Jobs' lead, but required exceptional talent of senior management and others. With Pixar, in what is almost an extended sidebar starring Jobs and John Lasseter, the story of its near-death (multiple times) and the path to billions is probably less known and has a fascination of its own. As with Apple, an \\"A team\\" of critical players (here, the brilliant animators and story tellers who finally persuaded Jobs that Pixar's future was animation, not products) was likely the difference between success and failure or an average existence.  The cast of characters from Apple and the industry is broad and interesting, in the cameos to more extended roles. Isaacson rightly gets credit for the notable exchanges regarding Jobs and Bill Gates, as one example. The well-known love/hate saga of relationships between Jobs and others must appear for at least a a couple dozen people discussed here alone.  The book is not rich on product details or how large companies run. This certainly differs from what a detailed bio on Gates would have to cover. Given that Isaacson is not a technical person, and the person and product drama would resonate with a larger audience, he appears to have made the right choice.  The book is a must for people in the field, and the fanboys, too.

1451648537|5.0|Apple Computer's founder Steve Jobs astounded the world with his creative vision and confounded people with his mercurial, often cruel personality. Best-selling biographer and media insider Walter Isaacson synthesizes the conflicting aspects, rooted deep within Jobs's psyche, of a man whose harsh and relentless demand for perfection at times threatened to overshadow his genius. Written at Jobs's request soon after his cancer diagnosis - but without his control or final say - Isaacson's book thoroughly dissects the Apple founder's life through extensive interviews and research. getAbstract recommends this definitive biography of a complicated, creative and ultimately world-changing persona.

0312641893|5.0|She was a cyborg, and she would never go to a ball.<br /><br />Cinder by Marissa Meyer is one outrageously amazing book! If you haven't read it yet, get on it. LIKE. RIGHT NOW. It's funny, touching, thought-provoking, fiercely compelling and insanely heartbreaking. Ridiculously well-written, taut, original, deeply emotional and gorgeously mesmerizing, it's the definition of a page-turner.<br /><br />  Linh Cinder is a cyborg, which of course means some of her body parts have been replaced with mechanical ones. It also means, she's a second-class citizen and she is treated as such. She lives with her \\"adopted family\\" in New Beijing, in the Eastern Commonwealth, and has to earn her keep working as a full-service mechanic at the Beijing's weekly market. The Earth's population, ravaged by Letumosis, was driven to the brink of extinction. No one knows the cause of this worldwide pandemic that already killed thousands of people. No one knows how to cure it, either.<br /><br />  Letumosis is not the only thing that Prince Kai has to worry about, though. There is also the threat of war against the Lunar (Moon) Empire hanging over the nation (no pun intended!). The evil Queen Levana has long been lusting over the blue planet. She wants Earth for herself, and she always gets everything she wants. She's powerful. She can manipulate the bioelectricity of people around her, casting a sort of glamour, making them see and feel whatever she chooses. She's despicable and ruthless, yet people can't resist her \\"charm\\". She's ugly and disgusting, yet all that others can see is a beautiful, trust-worthy, angel-like woman. And they follow her. And now she wants Prince Kai and Earth, and she's tired of waiting and negotiating.<br /><br />  Cinder's world is flipped inside-out when her beloved little sister, Peony, falls ill and is taken away to die in isolation. Her grief-stricken step-mother blames Cinder for her daughter's sickness. She sends Cinder away, volunteering her as a test subject for plague testing. But that's just the beginning of her problems. What she's about to discover will change not only her life, but also the lives of everyone around her, possibly the whole planet.<br /><br />  This book left me speechless.<br />When I first started reading, I was, like, \\"So OK, it looks like just another re-telling of the classic fairy tale with a futuristic cyborg twist\\". I was intrigued (I have a THING for anything clockwork), but not entirely sold yet. The first 20-30 pages is like that. The beginning of the story was great, but with Prince Kai visiting Cinder's mechanical shop, and then Cinder  day-dreaming about His Hotness, it didn't have the air of uniqueness about it. Not until Peony got sick. That's exactly when - forgive my French - sh** hits the fan (trust me, it's a very accurate description of how things go really bad from this moment onward). From that point in the story EVERYTHING you thought you knew about Cinderella is re-defined and re-envisioned. For the most part it still follows the simple set up of the original tale, but Meyer adds some seriously insane depth to it. She builds around it, gracefully weaving all the fascinating futuristic details and extraterrestrial elements into the plot, creating a complex, impressive, fantastically imagined world. It's mind-blowing.<br /><br />  Marissa Meyer created a truly unforgettable world. Her writing style is amazingly descriptive and enjoyable. She has such a fabulous imagination (and is not afraid to use it!). I can easily picture New Beijing in my head. I think the setting of the story couldn't be any more perfect. On top of the fabulous world-building there's also the savvy plot line. It's a mesmerizing blend of steampunk, fairy tale AND mystery. Some plot developments are easy to predict, but mostly you're just sitting there with your jaw on your knees and eyes bulging out in surprise. And even if you could predict everything that will happen, you'd still love reading about it- it's a real crowd-pleaser in terms of its readability.<br /><br />  I loved the characters. To say that they had more depth than the characters from the original fairy tale would be a major understatement. For once it's not about the silly ball, dancing with the shallow Prince or running away in a carriage made of pumpkin! The characters are real, their emotions are real, they have real problems, fears, struggles. There is a ball, but it's not the kind of ball you imagine when you think of Cinderella. There's a mechanical foot instead of a glass slipper and the dress is not only wrinkled, grease-stained and not in the least magical, but there is also a profound meaning to it. The Prince is not a brain-dead, spoiled little master with personality deficiency, who fancy sumptuous balls. He's an intelligent, good-hearted young man, who finds himself caught between duty and love. He has some insanely difficult decisions to make, and choosing a bride for himself is the least of his problems. Even the wicked step-mother is not entirely rid of human feelings. As much as I disliked her, she did have a few genuinely heart-breaking moments, when you could see a whole different layer of emotions shining through. My favorite character, though, was Cinder's adorable android friend, Iko. She was ROTFL-worthy, witty, supportive, loyal and just so.. cute! Her hilarious comments made for fantastic tension breakers. I really hope to see more of Iko in the next book of the Lunar Chronicles! Oh, and let's not forget about Queen Levana! Oh lord, what a gorgeously evil character she is! The White Witch and the Snow Queen combined wouldn't stand a chance! The whole concept of the Lunar Kingdom and the long lost Princess Selene reminded me of Sailor Moon - how could it not?! There are of course some major differences, but I'm fairly confident that Naoko Takeuchi's greatly enjoyable manga series inspired this breath-taking YA debut novel in some ways. And I LOVED IT!<br /><br />  To sum it all up: Cinder by Marissa Meyer is one helluva book! It's only the beginning of 2012 and yet I'm 100% sure this book will make it to my TOP 10 of 2012 list. I can't wait to learn more about Queen Levana and the mysterious Lunar Kingdom, and find out what will happen to Cinder, Nainsi and Prince Kai. I strongly recommend this read to everyone!!!oved the characters. To say that they had more depth than the characters from the original fairy tale would be a major understatement. 1451627289|3.0|I had no interest in reading this book - this is, until I discovered that it had beaten out my favorite book of the year (Ready Player One) in the Sci-Fi category for the 2011 GoodReads Choice Awards on GoodReads.com .  Then I started reading some reviews and thought I'd check out what all the hubbub was about.  I find this a difficult book to review, because on the one hand, the topic of time travel has been waaaay overdone.  And the topic of JFK had also been waaay overdone - many times over in fact.  But, the book itself was still a nice treat, in a lot of ways.  But, how do you prove to prospective book buyers that this is new and exciting fiction to read?  It's not.  Only Stephen King can make a book with a concept like this go somewhere.  It didn't go far, but it entertains.  Some reviews praised the book as a love story.  Okay...sure, but \\"Somewhere in Time\\" or \\"The Time Traveller's Wife\\" it's not.  Even as pure romance, there was a lot missing - and very poorly written sex scenes (especially when dealing with a virgin...c'mon...obviously from a man's ignorant perspective)!  No matter how good a writer Mr. King is, there was no saving those areas of the book.  But, Jake is a likable character - and so is Sadie.  I liked them together as a couple.  I liked reading about them dancing (and dancing plays a key role in this book - probably one of the biggest ones, which I thought was unique and sweet). Al = Doc Brown and Jake = Marty.  Yawwwwn!!!  And `The Butterfly Effect'??? Ummm...that's been done, too.  Give me something NEW to do with time travel, puleeeze!  When I read the Time Traveller's Wife, it blew my mind - it was an exciting take on time travel!!!  This seemed like manufactured time travel junk.  Sure, there were a few mysteries (JIMLA!) and interesting revelations at the end, but nothing as satisfying as I'd hoped to turn me around.  I enjoyed the ending.  Some reviews praised the 1950s-60s references.  Yes - these were well done.  I enjoyed reading the first 1/3 of the book which described a lot of the lifestyle in that era - how easy some things were (like, a degree from Oklahoma University by writing one easy multiple choice test) and how difficult others were (like, just getting around town without GPS!).  Some reviews praised the JFK assassination research.  Okay...sure, lots of good research and assumptions, but this was all boring as heck.  Lots of it I already knew, and the stuff I didn't, and most I found to be repetitive drivel.  I literally skipped every scene in the book that Oswald was in.  Didn't care, and it didn't make a difference.  Could've shaved off about 300 pages!  Really, the `event in history' to go back in time to change, could have been a lot of monumental moments in history.  Why did King pick this one?  King mentioned that he started to write this book back in the early 70s.  Too bad he didn't.  I think it would have been a better story back then (pre-Back to the Future, et al).

0385534639|5.0|\\"The circus arrives without warning. No announcements precede it. It is simply there, when yesterday it was not.\\"  The Night Circus is simply fantastic. Every aspect of the book is carefully contrived to fit the circus. The description is layered and brilliant, the characters are deep and alive, the plot is complex and subtle. It is the sort of book that looks rather plain at first blush, but then, when you glance away, seeps into your thoughts and your dreams.  The Night Circus is a rather difficult story to describe. It's about a boy and a girl, and a contest, and mostly about a circus. It's a little like The Prestige and a little like The Scorpio Races, but mostly it is like itself. This is not the sort of book that will be for everyone. It has a rather slow pace and takes longer to read than you might expect at 387 pages. There isn't a great deal of action and the voice of the story is unusual, third-person present omniscient, which basically means that you're watching rather than being the characters. In my opinion, everything about this story was perfect for this story. Anywhere else, I would probably hate it, but Morgenstern does such a wonderful job creating the atmosphere in this book that it almost seems as though there is no other way to do it.  The characters in this book were superb. Despite the odd style, I felt more connected to even the minor characters than I have to some first-person narrators. The ways they acted and interacted felt entirely real and believable. Marco and Celia especially had me biting my nails through half the book, and some of the characters had me guessing up till the very end.  If you like the styles of Guy Gavriel Kay, Maggie Stiefvater, and Audrey Niffenegger; if you like slow, subtle plots and games within games; if you like the gradual building of suspense and tension, then this is the book for you. I thought it was absolutely incredible. If you're not sure, give it a try - pretty soon you'll either love it or hate it. I love it.  \\"You think, as you walk away from Le Cirque des RÃªves and into the creeping dawn, that you felt more alive within the confines of the circus.  You are no longer quite certain which side of the fence is a dream.\\"

For instance the fact that the highly regarded Mr Blackmore is present and accounted for and this is from the days when he was still firmly ensconced in the rockin' and rollin' game - which means that for many who have been stoned since Osaka this is a no-brainer, they are just going to buy anything with their heroes name on it. So if you can name all the late 60's singles or projects that various Deep Purple members were a part of before forming the Mk 1 line up then.... why are you even reading this? Seeking validation? You already own it!!!<br /><br />But all jokes aside there are other factors to consider here and after the aforementioned Mr Blackmore there is the at least as legendary Ronnie James Dio on vox. I have read that this is the first properly shot concert footage of him (though am happy to be told I'm wrong on that score) and if so this leads this live gig to have somewhat of a historic value.<br /><br />The fact that there is also Cozy Powell on drums and that this was from an era when a lot of hard rock music was still fresh and new adds more lustre to the brew. While some reviewers have mocked the sound quality my copy doesn't seem to have any real issues. Perhaps it was some initial batch that had dodgy sound? The stage show is from the late 70's and this isn't KISS it must be said but seeing that electronic rainbow above, er, Rainbow, is kinda fun and it's actually refreshing to see such a down home honest stage show.<br /><br />All of the above are the positives about the actual gig. Negatives? Well, let me put it to you like this - here is the track listing;<br /><br />Kill the King - 4.42<br />Mistreated - 11.19<br />Sixteenth Century Greensleeves - 8.52<br />Catch the Rainbow - 18.11<br />Long Live Rock `n' Roll - 8.04<br />Man On The Silver Mountain - 16.25<br />Still I'm Sad - 27.33<br />Do You Close Your Eyes - 15.40<br /><br />Oh and I just double checked all that before posting this review and yep those are the correct running times. Now I'm all up for a (slightly) extended version in the live setting and a guitar solo and drum solo I'm not going to decry but.... well did you see those numbers? After playing it straight for the first tume they then take over eleven minutes to get through Mistreated, over eight minutes of improve for Greensleeves and thence meander over the eighteen minute mark for Catch The Rainbow. And as you can see from the above litany the fun doesn't stop there. Oh no, after the almost obscene brevity of only spending eight minutes to scurry through Long Live Rock `n' Roll they then spend over sixteen minutes of your life on the next track and over 27 minutes on Still I'm Sad. Now I know it was the 70's and this sort of gumph was in fashion with the heavy lidded set (lid, get it?) but heavens to betsy... And then the audience is barraged with over fifteen minutes of their life being taken up with the next tune. Now I don't have an issue with long tracks. I enjoy a bunch of `em from metal to classical but if you are going to do it then go somewhere with it. Not just flex your plectrum (ahem).<br /><br />Based on actual value for your time I'd have to rate this concert as squarely sub standard despite all the positive things I said at the start of this review. And if the producers were cheeky enough just to offer this then I'd give the thing two stars and watch the inevitable spluttering hate comments mount up. But what makes me give this an extra star is the bonus features. You get three video clips and these are great as they showcase the bands brilliance in a more fruitful setting from the commerciality of Long Live Rock `n' Roll to the more obtuse but no less greatness of Gates of Babylon. A photo gallery and interview footage round out the package and I note that this is often available quite cheaply so it's not a bad buy for the casual fan as long as they are aware of what they are getting.(slightly) extended version in the live setting and a guitar solo and drum solo I'm not going to decry but.... well did you see those numbers? There are many books about how to code in HTML and HTML5, but I'm not aware of that many that focus on the context -- what makes HTML5 similar to or different from other flavors of HTML; what are the strengths and weaknesses of HTML5; what problems was HTML5 designed to address; what are the alternatives to HTML5 (and their strengths and weaknesses); what other coding languages and tools make good partners to use with HTML5; strategically, how can you make intelligent plans to incorporate HTML5 in your own work or that of your enterprise; what types of products or projects are a good match for HTML5; what are currently HTML5 best practices; and expected future directions. There is more, of course. This short book is written in accessible language for people with some experience coding, but you do not need to be an expert to understand the content. The insights of this book would be especially valuable to managers who determine directions for programming projects, but are not necessarily coders themselves, as well as for people who are coders in other languages who are trying to decide if HTML5 is worth pursuing for their specific goals.

B001RCD2DW|5.0|AKG is known in professional audio circles as a serious microphone manufacturer whose all pro products continue to be manufactured in Vienna. It is also known for its spectral preferences for neutrality-with-warmth without sacrificing detail and musical honesty at either end of the spectrum. I still own an antiquated pair of AKG phones, the long-out-of-date 400's. I was shocked and delighted to discover that just like anything old and reliable, AKG has upgraded its innards without sacrificing the essence of what those old phones offered - an exceptionally comfortable, over the ear long-time listening experience.  If you're a studio or control room rat, you may not be able to use an open-backed pair of headphones because of feedback problems or ambient room environmental conditions, but for stage width and depth, sense of spread, and \\"air\\" these phones have only the woefully uncomfortable, fussy and now largely extinct STAX phones as anything like serious competition. When listening at low level or background, I can often turn off main speakers in a high resolution system and listen full range with no degradation in image and detail when using only the headphones. Comfortable they are, and although their self-adjusting suspension system hasn't seen any upgrade for over twenty years, there's been no reason to change it.  Unlike many other high-end professional open backed phones, these phones don't pinch the spectrum or exert overemphasis at frequency extremes just to sound \\"more hi-fi-ish.\\" They are honest. If what you've mastered just isn't there on end-use playback, they'll tell you that, instantly and intuitively. They aren't \\"cans with built-in tone controls\\" like many of their open-backed equals in the same general price range (or much higher). They don't pinch a full tutti or become irritatingly confused with complex mid-range multi-instrumental demands nor do they lose their voice at low levels. Furthermore, they never lose their sense of balance and compose...something a critical concert goer and amateur musician looks for when experiencing others' performances. To combine utter honesty with profound musicality, these phones are an unequaled bargain. Without having to re-wire them with any special fat wire, the extra cost of having a detachable cable is not only convenient but thoughtful -- given our throw-away world's disappearance of authentically skilled repair technicians. An excellent bargain, available at an Internet dealer's discount price fully over a hundred dollars from their walk-in store retail price. As Harmon International continues to shed reputable high end retail dealers in favor of pushing its Chinese junk technojewelry, perhaps the only place quality items such as these phones may continue to be available is with the few pro-end dealers and specialty On-line sources. The only downside to the AKG brand is that its Vienna-based manufacturing is seeing fewer and fewer new products to feed the insatiable professional, home-studio, and location recording market. Turning to China to produce pig fodder for tone deaf, tin-eared consumers and prosumers may be all in the present and foreseeable future for business venture survival, but with the cost to serious listeners of forcing them further into revery for the loss of musical discipline and performance honesty.  Not hard to drive, but also not for the underpowered mashed compression of any lossy playback medium, these phones don't present a problem for most headphone playback circuits on standard quarter inch phone- plug equipped devices.  If you have to go to a 3.5mm miniplug headphone source, you're already out of the range of what these can phones. And no, for \\"that market,\\" size isn't everything.

B004FGMDOQ|4.0|Jacob is your typical rich-kid teenager.  He's not particularly good at anything but doesn't need to try hard - his family has serious wealth.  A natural loner, Jacob's the kind of kid whose only friend is there as much for protection from other teens as he is to infuriate his parents (the guy's got green hair).  His only bond is with his grandfather, a dodgy old coot who lived a life of adventure in his younger days.  But no longer - Grandpa is suffering from paranoid dementia - he claims that monsters are coming to get him.  Jacob tries to calm him down, only to have his Grandpa butchered . . . by a monster, the stuff of Jacob's nightmares.  Grandpa's dying words to Jacob raise some odd questions, but Jacob is too shattered to address them immediately.  Reeling from grief, guilt and horror, Jacob receives a mysterious letter that indicates that there may be some truth to his Grandpa's more outlandish stories.  So Jacob and his less-than-commanding father trek to Wales to explore the land of Grandpa's youth.  And it is here that Jacob discovers Miss Peregrine's home for \\"peculiar\\" children.  These kids aren't just odd, and they aren't freaks.  But they can do things that normal kids can only dream of - but in an attempt to keep this spoiler-free that's all I'm going to say about these fantastic kids Jacob meets.  What ensues is part journey of discovery for Jacob, part horror story, as the monsters who hunted for Grandpa continue their quest for Miss Peregrine and Jacob.  Riggs keeps this wondrous story grounded in the real world of a Florida teenager, but he spices things up with a series of creepy photos of the peculiar kids from the early days of photography, and these photos have to be seen to be believed.  This book reads like the first novel in a new series - if so, it's an excellent setting of the table.  Jacob may not be the most thrilling protagonist, but he's believable and, when the chips are down, surprised to learn that he's just a bit heroic.  A goodread for kids 10 and up.

Scientists have discovered that the reason for war and suffering in the world is owed to love, and therefore, they created a cure for this disease that is administered once you turn 18. Alex.<br /><br />At first she's wary of him, I mean, if I'd spent my entire life believing that interaction with the opposite sex before the cure could kill you, I'd be wary too. 0451232577|4.0|A novel of love and war by master storyteller Ken Follett. Fall of Giants is the first in his new Century Trilogy, covering the beginnings of the twentieth century.<br /><br />We follow five families from several countries through the period right before World War I up to the mid 1920s. Viewpoints of people and politicians from Russia, Germany, Britain, America and other countries involved in WWI were distinct. We see glimpses of battlefield trenches, espionage, diplomacy strategies, love affairs, and women contributing to the war effort from home. I learned a great deal about the political intricacies of the war, class struggle (fall of the aristocracy and rise of the working class) and the women's suffragette movement. Much time is spent on the politics of war, and while I would have preferred more plot development, I respect the research that went into this historical tome.<br /><br />Each character is well-fleshed out and the plot nicely paced. I found the characters fascinating and distinct, but dwarfed by the ramifications of war. The love story between a British woman and German man was icing on the cake, but left me wanting for more information about them. My favorite character was a miner from Wales, William Williams (or Billy Twice). Two Russian brothers switch identities. The implications of that are fascinating. The cast of characters is nicely organized by country in the beginning of the book.<br /><br />Many reviews on Amazon scrutinize and criticize the book for stereotypical characters according to ethnic origin. I read the book purely for pleasure and enjoyed being entertained and instructed. Nothing can compare in my mind to Follett's brilliant [[ASIN:045123281X The Pillars of the Earth]], but perhaps I just prefer the medieval period. Still, Fall of Giants was compelling enough for me to want to read the next two installments in the trilogy.<br /><br />Reviewed by Holly Weiss, author of Crestmont

B002J8LVO0|4.0|Sounding positively triumphant without retaining the martial, strident, aggressive vibe that characterized much of the material from U2's previous release, \\"War\\", \\"Pride (In The Name Of Love)\\", driven by its boyuancy, conviction, and catchy, elemental hooks, was the first and only track to chart here in America upon the release of \\"The Unforgettable Fire\\", and made me wonder before purchasing it if the other tracks it contained would match the strongest ones on \\"War\\" in projecting a qualitatively anthemic feel.<br /><br />Unaware that \\"Pride\\" was something of an anomaly on this recording, I was a bit disoriented after listening to \\"TUF\\" all the way through the first time. Where were the passionate, ecstatic cluster of tracks I'd come to expect from each of the releases preceding \\"Unforgettable Fire\\"? And while not an occurrence with a universal sweep, what was the deal w/r/t the subdued, moody, heavily atmospheric texture weaving through seemingly every cut but \\"Pride\\" and \\"Wire\\"? I'd never heard of a transitional recording before, and could only identify \\"Unforgettable Fire\\" as being one well in hindsight. Did this release mark a very long downturn for the fervent, wide-eyed, full-of-indie-cred U2, or was the band making a positive effort to alter some of the dynamics of their sound to evolve without necessarily losing their trademark level of intensity and passion?<br /><br />No real consistent universal thread runs through this CD other than a pronounced bent toward orchestration; lyrical content and musical arrangement are not unified by one overarching theme. While some tracks positively flow despite the new level of ambience brought to bear on the production values (\\"A Sort Of Homecoming\\", the conventional \\"Pride\\", the title track, \\"Bad\\" and \\"Indian Summer Sky\\")others are more like introspective sketches that never resolve themselves into tracks with any sort of progression (\\"Promenade\\", \\"4th of July\\", and \\"Elvis Presley In America\\"), perhaps due to the improvisational nature of the latter two and the lack of summative resolution w/r/t to the first. And it isn't as if U2 and the Eno/Lanois production team didn't have other material to work with; \\"Love Comes Tumbling\\", a down-tempo cut that nevertheless possesses a slightly aggressive insistence thanks to some nifty arpeggiated guitar work and popping bassline could've been inserted between \\"The Unforgettable Fire\\" and \\"Promenade\\", while the rippling, explosive crescendo of Edge's guitar work and joyful, invocational quality of Bono's vocals make \\"The Three Sunrises\\" one of their most ecstatic songs, worthy of inclusion between \\"Indian Summer Sky\\" and \\"Elvis Presley In America\\". Neither track survived the final cut; both made their respective appearances on \\"The Unforgettable Fire\\" single and their \\"live\\" EP, \\"Wide Awake In America\\", but would've been more strongly situated here.<br /><br />Nevertheless, there's enough about \\"The Unforgettable Fire\\" that redeems it as a recording while also refining and redefining U2's overall approach to musical arrangement. While not one of their more vital releases, it still bristles with an introspective intensity that informs at least three tracks that still make the cut in concert, including two (\\"Pride\\" and \\"Bad\\") that are essential live staples. Recommended.

What Lupton gives us is a tale of the relationship between two sisters all wrapped up in a mystery. Rather than taking away from either story, these two stories fit well together and keep the reader involved. Bee flies to London as her sister is reported missing, only to have the police discover her sister's body and to have the coroner declare it as a suicide. Bee does not believe Tess would commit suicide and proceeds to try to prove otherwise. The story is told in the form of letters to her sister and Bee's ultimate recollection of events. I found both stories captivating and engrossing. The characters were excellently portrayed and believable. The relationship story had the right mix of nostalgia and poignancy. The mystery was excellent, complete with all the right twists and turns and an ending that was surprising on more than one level. Great debut by Ms. Lupton and I am looking forward to reading her next book.

B005LAIGMI|3.0|Traditionally, January is the time that most film studios tend to dump the films that they know aren't very good but that they're hoping they can still make some money off of before releasing them on DVD.  As such, I tend to approach films differently in January than I do most months of the year.  I go in with lowered expectations and yet, often times, I'm still surprised by how disappointing most of the films in January truly are.  However, this year, I'm surprised and happy to say that a genuinely entertaining film has actually been released in January and that film is Steven Soderbergh's Haywire.  In Haywire, Mixed Martial Artist Gina Carano plays Mallory Kane, an ex-Marine who is now working as a covert operative for a security firm owned by her ex-boyfriend (Ewan McGregor).  After rescuing a hostage in Barcelona, Mallory is immediately sent on another assignment to Dublin.  However, once she gets to Dublin and meets up with another enigmatic operative (Michael Fassbender), she quickly discovers that she's been set up and somebody wants her dead.  The rest of the film deals with her efforts to discover who has set her up and why and then getting her own brand of violent revenge on her enemies.  At this point, Steven Soderbergh could pretty much make this type of film in his sleep and, to a certain extent, Haywire feels a lot like Soderbergh on autopilot.  Fortunately, Soderbergh on autopilot is still usually more interesting than most other American directors working today.  He's using his usual tricks here (frequent flashbacks, a jazzy score, and an emphasis on style over substance) but he uses them well and the end result is an entertaining and fast-paced 90 minute film.  Gina Carano, to be honest, isn't much of an actress but she's a great fighter and the scenes where she's literally fighting for her life are all the more exciting because her amazing moves are not the result of CGI but her own natural abilities.  Ewan McGregor, Michael Fassbender, and Bill Paxton are all well-cast and Antonio Banderas has a lot of fun with his cameo appearance.  The final result is a genuinely entertaining film that's considerably better than the usual January cinematic product.

As it's DVD only I'll urge them to move it to streaming so others might happen on it.  I grew deeply interested in the Holocaust maybe fifteen years ago while doing an edit on a friends novel, set in the time.  She wrote one chapter of the charaters ride in a boxcar to Auschwitz that I was reminded of each time one of the frequent freight trains passed by our house.  I knew of course what had happened before, but it personalized it, as one human beigns point of view, (especially since he knew what awaited them and many of the other didn't}  I started seeking out books written by survivors and the liberators and the ghastly black and white (later color) pictures took on a new life, about people.  This movie does the same.  I knew nothing about it but the short summary and wasn't sure what to expect, and was drawn in immedidatly.  Sure, there are inaccuracies.  The children would have been indoctrinated to a degree, the daughter most of all.  The boy is very much a child of war, witness the way his friends pretended a realistic view of how war is carried out.  And the husband, knowing his wife was uncertain and not completely patriotic, would have hid it better or been called and sent to the front himself.  But it isn't about that, its about the viseral experience.  When they arrive at the new house, with armed guards and a gate, when they see the stark almost prisonlike interior, you know what sort of a place they are.  Bruno is a dreamer, and dreamers tend to shut out certain perceptions.  And he's lonely.  His new friend knows what the place is, and spends time sitting alone.  Maybe in a different world they'd be very much alike.  And he still has his father.  You can see the panic starting to surface when his father dissapears.  The two boys make a connection through childhood with this magical spark.  Dreamers, in a harsh, terrible world,often pay the price of dreaming. It's been around nearly 60 years as of this writing and I've been alive for many of those years but couldn't say I took the time to see this Disney 'classic'. After viewing it last night I have to say it is 'classic Disney'- what the Disney company USED to be. CLASSICAL music permeates it which is so different than the mainstream pop/contempo tunes in newer Disney fair. What I REALLY like (love) is how innocent the children are. There are no wisecracks, there is honest respect for parents and there's just a certain sweetness that never comes across in the newer films. Nemo (Finding) perhaps has touches of this innocence characteristic but nothing like sweet little Michael who clings to his teddy bear.  Another thing I love about this film is the animation. It's beautiful and, again, classic Disney. You can tell a lot of work and care went into every drawing and coloring. The portrayal as women as these subservient, empty headed objects is a bit much. The use of classical instrumentation for EVERY SINGLE CHARACTER MOVEMENT is barely tolerable. It's weird to think people used to think like that. B0010X8NF0|5.0|I am not particularly a fan of Anderson - I loved Rushmore, but was bored by both The Life Aquatic and The Royal Tenenbaums. I am, however, a fan of good cinema. To me The Darjeeling Limited demonstrates both the beauty and simplicity of the cinematic form, but more than that, it demonstrates potential. It demonstrates what is possible, not only in terms of the creative form, but also what the Hollywood machine; the accountants, backers, producers and studio executives (those outside of the artistic process) are capable of agreeing to finance. This is the real brilliance of this project.<br /><br />In terms of the actual cinematic experience, the sets, costumes, music, cinematography, dialogue and performances are about as good as I have seen in a LONG while; this is such a very coherent project which is ultimately the antithesis of what the potential viewer can engage with if they head down to their local rental shop. I think this is a film even detractors should watch once, because I sincerely believe that to anyone who watches it, their frame-of-reference will be shifted, altered and expanded. 1451648537|5.0|This book is positioned at the rare nexus of a brilliant biographer and an amazing subject. I've read several of Isaacson's biographies (Ben Franklin, Albert Einstein), and they never disappoint. He has that rare gift that I have always appreciated in a history teacher of being able to recount the past as an enthralling, engaging story. But with Steve Jobs he completely outdoes himself. The book moves along at a torrid pace. It is not told completely chronologically; but rather as a series of stories about different events. He tells the story of the iPod, then he tells the story of Pixar being sold to Disney, then he tells the story of the early failures of Jobs' health. All three stories overlap in time, but are incredible narratives in their own right.  I loved reading about why the Beatles did not permit the sale of their music on iTunes, how Jobs' took the idea of a GUI from Xerox (and Xerox management didn't even mind), the ideological battle over an integrated but closed architecture versus an open but inelegant design, how Job's set aside the creation of the iPad in order to focus on the iPhone.  I also enjoyed getting to know what made Jobs tick. He is portrayed with all his flaws; and they are many. This man who changed so much about our world was neither a good boss nor a good husband/father. And yet his employees and his family loved him.  I heartily recommend this book.
